id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table symbol">symbol</a></th><td>Hydrogen-2,<sup>2</sup>H or D</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Neutron_number" title="Neutron number">Neutrons</a></th><td>1</td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a style="display:none">7000201355321274500&#9824;</span>2.013<span style="margin-left:.25em;">553</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">212</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">745</span>(40)</span> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unified_atomic_mass_unit" title="Unified atomic mass unit">u</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">Spin</a></th><td>1<sup>+</sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Excess_energy" title="Excess energy">Excess energy</a></th><td>13135.720&#177; 0.001 <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt" title="Electronvolt">keV</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">Binding energy</a></th><td>2224.52&#177; 0.20 keV</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/Table_of_nuclides" title="Table of nuclides">Complete table of nuclides</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Deuterium</b> (or <b>hydrogen-2</b>, symbol <b><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>D<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></b> or <b><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></b>, also known as <b>heavy hydrogen</b>) is one of two <a href="/wiki/Stable_isotope_ratio" title="Stable isotope ratio">stable isotopes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> (the other being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-1" title="Hydrogen-1">protium</a>, or hydrogen-1). The <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">nucleus</a> of deuterium, called a <b>deuteron</b>, contains one <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">proton</a> and one <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a>, whereas the far more common protium has no neutron in the nucleus. Deuterium has a <a href="/wiki/Natural_abundance" title="Natural abundance">natural abundance</a> in Earth\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oceans" title="Oceans">oceans</a> of about one <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atom</a> in <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003642000000000000&#9824;</span>6420</span> of hydrogen. Thus deuterium accounts for approximately 0.0156% (or, on a mass basis, 0.0312%) of all the naturally occurring hydrogen in the oceans, while protium accounts for more than 99.98%. The abundance of deuterium changes slightly from one kind of natural water to another (see <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Standard_Mean_Ocean_Water" title="Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water">Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water</a>).\n</p><p>The deuterium isotope\'s name is formed from the Greek <i>deuteros</i>, meaning "second", to denote the two particles composing the nucleus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diplogen_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-diplogen-2">[2]</a></sup> Deuterium was discovered and named in 1931 by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Urey" title="Harold Urey">Harold Urey</a>. When the neutron was discovered in 1932, this made the nuclear structure of deuterium obvious, and Urey won the Nobel Prize in 1934. Soon after deuterium\'s discovery, Urey and others produced samples of "<a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a>" in which the deuterium content had been highly concentrated.\n</p><p>Deuterium is destroyed in the interiors of stars faster than it is produced. Other natural processes are thought to produce only an insignificant amount of deuterium. Nearly all deuterium found in nature was produced in the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> 13.8 billion years ago, as the basic or primordial ratio of hydrogen-1 to deuterium (about 26 atoms of deuterium per million hydrogen atoms) has its origin from that time. This is the ratio found in the gas giant planets, such as Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diplogen_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-diplogen-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature2_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature2-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hersant_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hersant-4">[4]</a></sup> However, other astronomical bodies are found to have different ratios of deuterium to hydrogen-1. This is thought to be a result of natural isotope separation processes that occur from solar heating of ices in comets. Like the water cycle in Earth\'s weather, such heating processes may enrich deuterium with respect to protium. The analysis of deuterium/protium ratios in comets found results very similar to the mean ratio in Earth\'s oceans (156 atoms of deuterium per million hydrogens). This reinforces theories that much of Earth\'s ocean water is of cometary origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature2_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-nature2-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hersant_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hersant-4">[4]</a></sup>  The deuterium/protium ratio of the comet 67P/Churyumov-Gerasimenko, as measured by the <a href="/wiki/Rosetta_(spacecraft)" title="Rosetta (spacecraft)">Rosetta space probe</a>, is about three times that of earth water. This figure is the highest yet measured in a comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencemag.org_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencemag.org-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Deuterium/protium ratios thus continue to be an active topic of research in both astronomy and climatology.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/220px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/330px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg/440px-Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_discharge_tube.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deuterium discharge tube</div></div></div>\n<p>Deuterium is frequently represented by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_symbol" title="Chemical symbol">chemical symbol</a> D. Since it is an isotope of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> with <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> 2, it is also represented by <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. <a href="/wiki/International_Union_of_Pure_and_Applied_Chemistry" title="International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry">IUPAC</a> allows both D and <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, although <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is preferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> A distinct chemical symbol is used for convenience because of the isotope\'s common use in various scientific processes. Also, its large mass difference with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-1" title="Hydrogen-1">protium</a> (<sup>1</sup>H) (deuterium has a mass of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000201410199999999&#9824;</span>2.014<span style="margin-left:.25em;">102</span>&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unified_atomic_mass_unit" title="Unified atomic mass unit">u</a></span>, compared to the <a href="/wiki/Mean" title="Mean">mean</a> hydrogen <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000100794700000000&#9824;</span>1.007<span style="margin-left:.25em;">947</span>&#160;u</span>, and protium\'s mass of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000100782500000000&#9824;</span>1.007<span style="margin-left:.25em;">825</span>&#160;u</span>) confers non-negligible chemical dissimilarities with protium-containing compounds, whereas the isotope weight ratios within other chemical elements are largely insignificant in this regard.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spectroscopy">Spectroscopy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Spectroscopy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> the energy levels of electrons in atoms depend on the <a href="/wiki/Reduced_mass" title="Reduced mass">reduced mass</a> of the system of electron and nucleus. For the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_atom" title="Hydrogen atom">hydrogen atom</a>, the role of reduced mass is most simply seen in the <a href="/wiki/Bohr_model" title="Bohr model">Bohr model</a> of the atom, where the reduced mass appears in a simple calculation of the <a href="/wiki/Rydberg_constant" title="Rydberg constant">Rydberg constant</a> and Rydberg equation, but the reduced mass also appears in the <a href="/wiki/Schr%C3%B6dinger_equation" title="Schr&#246;dinger equation">Schr&#246;dinger equation</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Dirac_equation" title="Dirac equation">Dirac equation</a> for calculating atomic energy levels.\n</p><p>The reduced mass of the system in these equations is close to the mass of a single electron, but differs from it by a small amount about equal to the ratio of mass of the electron to the atomic nucleus. For hydrogen, this amount is about 1837/1836, or 1.000545, and for deuterium it is even smaller: 3671/3670, or 1.0002725. The energies of spectroscopic lines for deuterium and light hydrogen (hydrogen-1) therefore differ by the ratios of these two numbers, which is 1.000272. The wavelengths of all deuterium spectroscopic lines are shorter than the corresponding lines of light hydrogen, by a factor of 1.000272. In astronomical observation, this corresponds to a blue Doppler shift of 0.000272 times the speed of light, or 81.6&#160;km/s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The differences are much more pronounced in vibrational spectroscopy such as <a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy" title="Infrared spectroscopy">infrared spectroscopy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Raman_spectroscopy" title="Raman spectroscopy">Raman spectroscopy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and in rotational spectra such as <a href="/wiki/Microwave_spectroscopy" title="Microwave spectroscopy">microwave spectroscopy</a> because the <a href="/wiki/Reduced_mass" title="Reduced mass">reduced mass</a> of the deuterium is markedly higher than that of protium. In <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy">nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy</a>, deuterium has a very different <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">NMR</a> frequency (e.g. 61&#160;MHz when protium is at 400&#160;MHz) and is much less sensitive. Deuterated solvents are usually used in protium NMR to prevent the solvent from overlapping with the signal, although <a href="/wiki/Deuterium_NMR" title="Deuterium NMR">deuterium NMR</a> on its own right is also possible.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Big Bang nucleosynthesis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a></div>\n<p>Deuterium is thought to have played an important role in setting the number and ratios of the elements that were formed in the Big Bang. Combining thermodynamics and the changes brought about by cosmic expansion, one can calculate the fraction of protons and neutrons based on the temperature at the point that the universe cooled enough to allow formation of nuclei. This calculation indicates seven protons for every neutron at the beginning of nucleogenesis, a ratio that would remain stable even after nucleogenesis was over. This fraction was in favor of protons initially, primarily because the lower mass of the proton favored their production. As the universe expanded, it cooled. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_neutron" title="Free neutron">Free neutrons</a> and protons are less stable than helium nuclei, and the protons and neutrons had a strong energetic reason to form helium-4. However, forming helium-4 requires the intermediate step of forming deuterium.\n</p><p>Through much of the few minutes after the big bang during which nucleosynthesis could have occurred, the temperature was high enough that the mean energy per particle was greater than the binding energy of weakly bound deuterium; therefore any deuterium that was formed was immediately destroyed. This situation is known as the <b>deuterium bottleneck</b>. The bottleneck delayed formation of any helium-4 until the universe became cool enough to form deuterium (at about a temperature equivalent to 100 keV). At this point, there was a sudden burst of element formation (first deuterium, which immediately fused to helium). However, very shortly thereafter, at twenty minutes after the Big Bang, the universe became too cool for any further nuclear fusion and nucleosynthesis to occur. At this point, the elemental abundances were nearly fixed, with the only change as some of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">radioactive</a> products of big bang nucleosynthesis (such as <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>) decay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The deuterium bottleneck in the formation of helium, together with the lack of stable ways for helium to combine with hydrogen or with itself (there are no stable nuclei with mass numbers of five or eight) meant that an insignificant amount of carbon, or any elements heavier than carbon, formed in the Big Bang. These elements thus required formation in stars. At the same time, the failure of much nucleogenesis during the Big Bang ensured that there would be plenty of hydrogen in the later universe available to form long-lived stars, such as our Sun.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abundance">Abundance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Abundance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Deuterium occurs in trace amounts naturally as deuterium <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a>, written <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span><sub>2</sub> or D<sub>2</sub>, but most natural occurrence in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> is bonded with a typical <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> atom, a gas called <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_deuteride" title="Hydrogen deuteride">hydrogen deuteride</a> (HD or <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The existence of deuterium on Earth, elsewhere in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_system" title="Solar system">solar system</a> (as confirmed by planetary probes), and in the spectra of <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">stars</a>, is also an important datum in <a href="/wiki/Physical_cosmology" title="Physical cosmology">cosmology</a>. Gamma radiation from ordinary nuclear fusion dissociates deuterium into protons and neutrons, and there are no known natural processes other than the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang_nucleosynthesis" title="Big Bang nucleosynthesis">Big Bang nucleosynthesis</a>, which might have produced deuterium at anything close to its observed natural abundance (deuterium is produced by the rare <a href="/wiki/Cluster_decay" title="Cluster decay">cluster decay</a>, and occasional absorption of naturally occurring neutrons by light hydrogen, but these are trivial sources). There is thought to be little deuterium in the interior of the Sun and other stars, as at temperatures there <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion_reaction" title="Nuclear fusion reaction">nuclear fusion reactions</a> that consume deuterium happen much faster than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton-proton_reaction" title="Proton-proton reaction">proton-proton reaction</a> that creates deuterium. However, deuterium persists in the outer solar atmosphere at roughly the same concentration as in Jupiter, and this has probably been unchanged since the origin of the Solar System. The natural abundance of deuterium seems to be a very similar fraction of hydrogen, wherever hydrogen is found, unless there are obvious processes at work that concentrate it.\n</p><p>The existence of deuterium at a low but constant primordial fraction in all hydrogen is another one of the arguments in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Big_Bang" title="Big Bang">Big Bang</a> theory over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steady_State_theory" title="Steady State theory">Steady State theory</a> of the universe. The observed ratios of hydrogen to helium to deuterium in the universe are difficult to explain except with a Big Bang model. It is estimated that the abundances of deuterium have not evolved significantly since their production about 13.8 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Measurements of Milky Way galactic deuterium from ultraviolet spectral analysis show a ratio of as much as 23 atoms of deuterium per million hydrogen atoms in undisturbed gas clouds, which is only 15% below the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/WMAP" title="WMAP">WMAP</a> estimated primordial ratio of about 27 atoms per million from the Big Bang. This has been interpreted to mean that less deuterium has been destroyed in star formation in our galaxy than expected, or perhaps deuterium has been replenished by a large in-fall of primordial hydrogen from outside the galaxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> In space a few hundred light years from the Sun, deuterium abundance is only 15 atoms per million, but this value is presumably influenced by differential adsorption of deuterium onto carbon dust grains in interstellar space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The abundance of deuterium in the atmosphere of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> has been directly measured by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo_space_probe" title="Galileo space probe">Galileo space probe</a> as 26 atoms per million hydrogen atoms. ISO-SWS observations find 22 atoms per million hydrogen atoms in Jupiter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> and this abundance is thought to represent close to the primordial solar system ratio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hersant_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hersant-4">[4]</a></sup> This is about 17% of the terrestrial deuterium-to-hydrogen ratio of 156 deuterium atoms per million hydrogen atoms.\n</p><p>Cometary bodies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comet_Hale-Bopp" title="Comet Hale-Bopp">Comet Hale-Bopp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley\'s Comet">Halley\'s Comet</a> have been measured to contain relatively more deuterium (about 200 atoms D per million hydrogens), ratios which are enriched with respect to the presumed protosolar nebula ratio, probably due to heating, and which are similar to the ratios found in Earth seawater. The recent measurement of deuterium amounts of 161 atoms D per million hydrogen in Comet <a href="/wiki/103P/Hartley" title="103P/Hartley">103P/Hartley</a> (a former <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> object), a ratio almost exactly that in Earth\'s oceans, emphasizes the theory that Earth\'s surface water may be largely comet-derived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature2_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-nature2-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hersant_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Hersant-4">[4]</a></sup> Most recently the deuterium/protium (D/H) ratio of <a href="/wiki/67P/Churyumov%E2%80%93Gerasimenko" title="67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko">67P/Churyumov&#8211;Gerasimenko</a> as measured by <i>Rosetta</i> is about three times that of Earth water, a figure that is high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencemag.org_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-sciencemag.org-5">[5]</a></sup> This has caused renewed interest in suggestions that Earth\'s water may be partly of asteroidal origin.\n</p><p>Deuterium has also observed to be concentrated over the mean solar abundance in other terrestrial planets, in particular Mars and Venus.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water#Production" title="Heavy water">Heavy water &#167;&#160;Production</a></div>\n<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">February 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Deuterium is produced for industrial, scientific and military purposes, by starting with ordinary water&#8212;a small fraction of which is naturally-occurring <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">heavy water</a>&#8212;and then separating out the heavy water by the <a href="/wiki/Girdler_sulfide_process" title="Girdler sulfide process">Girdler sulfide process</a>, distillation, or other methods.\n</p><p>In theory, deuterium for heavy water could be created in a nuclear reactor, but separation from ordinary water is the cheapest bulk production process.\n</p><p>The world\'s leading supplier of deuterium was <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_of_Canada_Limited" title="Atomic Energy of Canada Limited">Atomic Energy of Canada Limited</a>, in Canada, until 1997, when the last heavy water plant was shut down. Canada uses heavy water as a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_moderator" title="Neutron moderator">neutron moderator</a> for the operation of the <a href="/wiki/CANDU_reactor" title="CANDU reactor">CANDU reactor</a> design.\n</p><p>Another major producer of heavy water is India. All but one of India\'s atomic energy plants are pressurised heavy water plants, which use natural (i.e., not enriched) uranium. India has eight (seven are in operation) heavy water plants, six (five) based on D-H exchange in ammonia gas and two plants extract deuterium from natural water in a process that uses hydrogen sulphide gas at high pressure.\n</p><p>While India is self-sufficient in heavy water for its own use, India now also exports reactor-grade heavy water.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties">Properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The physical properties of deuterium compounds can exhibit significant <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_isotope_effect" title="Kinetic isotope effect">kinetic isotope effects</a> and other physical and chemical property differences from the hydrogen analogs. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D2O" title="D2O">D<sub>2</sub>O</a>, for example, is more <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscous</a> than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/H2O" title="H2O">H<sub>2</sub>O</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Chemically, there are differences in bond energy and length for compounds of heavy hydrogen isotopes compared to normal hydrogen, which are larger than the isotopic differences in any other element. Bonds involving deuterium and <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> are somewhat stronger than the corresponding bonds in hydrogen, and these differences are enough to cause significant changes in biological reactions. Pharmaceutical firms are interested in the fact that deuterium is harder to remove from carbon than hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Deuterium can replace the normal hydrogen in water molecules to form heavy water (D<sub>2</sub>O), which is about 10.6% denser than normal water (so that ice made from it sinks in ordinary water). Heavy water is slightly toxic in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotic" title="Eukaryotic">eukaryotic</a> animals, with 25% substitution of the body water causing cell division problems and sterility, and 50% substitution causing death by cytotoxic syndrome (bone marrow failure and gastrointestinal lining failure). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prokaryotic" title="Prokaryotic">Prokaryotic</a> organisms, however, can survive and grow in pure heavy water, though they develop slowly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Despite this toxicity, consumption of heavy water under normal circumstances does not pose a <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water#Toxicity" title="Heavy water">health threat</a> to humans. It is estimated that a 70&#160;kg (154&#160;lb) person might drink 4.8 litres (1.3&#160;US&#160;gal) of heavy water without serious consequences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Small doses of heavy water (a few grams in humans, containing an amount of deuterium comparable to that normally present in the body) are routinely used as harmless metabolic tracers in humans and animals.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_properties">Quantum properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Quantum properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The deuteron has <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quantum_spin" title="Quantum spin">spin</a> +1 ("triplet") and is thus a <a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">boson</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance">NMR</a> frequency of deuterium is significantly different from common light hydrogen. <a href="/wiki/Infrared_spectroscopy" title="Infrared spectroscopy">Infrared spectroscopy</a> also easily differentiates many deuterated compounds, due to the large difference in IR absorption frequency seen in the vibration of a chemical bond containing deuterium, versus light hydrogen. The two stable isotopes of hydrogen can also be distinguished by using <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a>.\n</p><p>The triplet deuteron nucleon is barely bound at E<sub>B</sub> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6987357285356601000&#9824;</span>2.23&#160;MeV</span>, and none of the higher energy states are bound. The singlet deuteron is a virtual state, with a negative binding energy of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6985961305892199999&#9824;</span>~60&#160;keV</span>. There is no such stable particle, but this virtual particle transiently exists during neutron-proton inelastic scattering, accounting for the unusually large neutron scattering cross-section of the proton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Nuclear_properties_.28the_deuteron.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_properties_(the_deuteron)">Nuclear properties (the deuteron)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nuclear properties (the deuteron)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deuteron_mass_and_radius">Deuteron mass and radius</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Deuteron mass and radius">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proton_radius_puzzle" title="Proton radius puzzle">Proton radius puzzle</a></div>\n<p>The nucleus of deuterium is called a <b>deuteron</b>. It has a mass of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000201355321274500&#9824;</span>2.013<span style="margin-left:.25em;">553</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">212</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">745</span>(40)&#160;u</span> (equal to 1875.612 928(12) MeV)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Charge_radius" title="Charge radius">charge radius</a> of the deuteron is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6985214130000000000&#9824;</span>2.1413(25)&#160;<a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fm</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Like the <a href="/wiki/Proton#Charge_radius" title="Proton">proton radius</a>, measurements using <a href="/wiki/Muon" title="Muon">muonic</a> deuterium produce a <a href="/wiki/Statistical_significance" title="Statistical significance">significantly</a> smaller result: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6985212562000000000&#9824;</span>2.125<span style="margin-left:.25em;">62</span>(78)&#160;<a href="/wiki/Femtometre" title="Femtometre">fm</a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This is 6&#963; less than the accepted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CODATA" title="CODATA">CODATA</a> 2014 value, measured using electrons, and confirms the unresolved proton charge radius anomaly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spin_and_energy">Spin and energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Spin and energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Deuterium is one of only five stable <a href="/wiki/Nuclide" title="Nuclide">nuclides</a> with an odd number of protons and an odd number of neutrons. (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_lithium" title="Isotopes of lithium"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Li<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_boron" title="Isotopes of boron"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">10</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>B<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_nitrogen" title="Isotopes of nitrogen"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">14</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>N<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_tantalum" title="Isotopes of tantalum"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">180m</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Ta<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a>; also, the long-lived radioactive nuclides <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">40</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>K<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_vanadium" title="Isotopes of vanadium"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">50</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>V<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_lanthanum" title="Isotopes of lanthanum"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">138</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>La<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a>, <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_lutetium" title="Isotopes of lutetium"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">176</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>Lu<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> occur naturally.) Most odd-odd nuclei are unstable with respect to <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">beta decay</a>, because the decay products are even-even, and are therefore more strongly bound, due to <a href="/wiki/Semi-empirical_mass_formula#Pairing_term" title="Semi-empirical mass formula">nuclear pairing effects</a>. Deuterium, however, benefits from having its proton and neutron coupled to a spin-1 state, which gives a stronger nuclear attraction; the corresponding spin-1 state does not exist in the two-neutron or two-proton system, due to the <a href="/wiki/Pauli_exclusion_principle" title="Pauli exclusion principle">Pauli exclusion principle</a> which would require one or the other identical particle with the same spin to have some other different quantum number, such as <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum_operator" title="Angular momentum operator">orbital angular momentum</a>. But orbital angular momentum of either particle gives a lower <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">binding energy</a> for the system, primarily due to increasing distance of the particles in the steep gradient of the nuclear force. In both cases, this causes the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diproton" title="Diproton">diproton</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dineutron" title="Dineutron">dineutron</a> nucleus to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unstable" title="Unstable">unstable</a>.\n</p><p>The proton and neutron making up deuterium can be <a href="/wiki/Dissociation_(chemistry)" title="Dissociation (chemistry)">dissociated</a> through <a href="/wiki/Neutral_current" title="Neutral current">neutral current</a> interactions with <a href="/wiki/Neutrino" title="Neutrino">neutrinos</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Cross_section_(physics)" title="Cross section (physics)">cross section</a> for this interaction is comparatively large, and deuterium was successfully used as a neutrino target in the <a href="/wiki/Sudbury_Neutrino_Observatory" title="Sudbury Neutrino Observatory">Sudbury Neutrino Observatory</a> experiment.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Isospin_singlet_state_of_the_deuteron">Isospin singlet state of the deuteron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Isospin singlet state of the deuteron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Due to the similarity in mass and nuclear properties between the proton and neutron, they are sometimes considered as two symmetric types of the same object, a <a href="/wiki/Nucleon" title="Nucleon">nucleon</a>. While only the proton has an electric charge, this is often negligible due to the weakness of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_interaction" title="Electromagnetic interaction">electromagnetic interaction</a> relative to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_nuclear_interaction" title="Strong nuclear interaction">strong nuclear interaction</a>. The symmetry relating the proton and neutron is known as <a href="/wiki/Isospin" title="Isospin">isospin</a> and denoted <i>I</i> (or sometimes <i>T</i>).\n</p><p>Isospin is an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SU(2)" title="SU(2)">SU(2)</a> symmetry, like ordinary <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a>, so is completely analogous to it. The proton and neutron form an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin_doublet" title="Spin doublet">isospin doublet</a>, with a <a href="/wiki/Spin-%C2%BD" title="Spin-&#189;">"down" state</a> (&#8595;) being a neutron, and an <a href="/wiki/Spin-%C2%BD" title="Spin-&#189;">"up" state</a> (&#8593;) being a proton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalm_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalm-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A pair of nucleons can either be in an antisymmetric state of isospin called <a href="/wiki/Singlet_state" title="Singlet state">singlet</a>, or in a symmetric state called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin_triplet" title="Spin triplet">triplet</a>. In terms of the "down" state and "up" state, the singlet is\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}{\\Big (}|\\uparrow \\downarrow \\rangle -|\\downarrow \\uparrow \\rangle {\\Big )}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo maxsize="1.623em" minsize="1.623em">(</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8593;<!-- &#8593; -->&#8595;<!-- &#8595; --></mo>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8595;<!-- &#8595; -->&#8593;<!-- &#8593; --></mo>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo maxsize="1.623em" minsize="1.623em">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}{\\Big (}|\\uparrow \\downarrow \\rangle -|\\downarrow \\uparrow \\rangle {\\Big )}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}{\\Big (}|\\uparrow \\downarrow \\rangle -|\\downarrow \\uparrow \\rangle {\\Big )}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e346fcb603a994e6bd47c8aee6ef2f913247547b" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:19.24ex; height:6.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>This is a nucleus with one proton and one neutron, i.e. a deuterium nucleus. The triplet is\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\left({\\begin{array}{ll}|\\uparrow \\uparrow \\rangle \\\\{\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}(|\\uparrow \\downarrow \\rangle +|\\downarrow \\uparrow \\rangle )\\\\|\\downarrow \\downarrow \\rangle \\end{array}}\\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mtable columnalign="left left" columnspacing="1em" rowspacing="4pt">\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8593;<!-- &#8593; -->&#8593;<!-- &#8593; --></mo>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<msqrt>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</msqrt>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8593;<!-- &#8593; -->&#8595;<!-- &#8595; --></mo>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8595;<!-- &#8595; -->&#8593;<!-- &#8593; --></mo>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n<mtr>\n<mtd>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">|</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">&#8595;<!-- &#8595; -->&#8595;<!-- &#8595; --></mo>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">&#10217;<!-- &#10217; --></mo>\n</mtd>\n</mtr>\n</mtable>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\left({\\begin{array}{ll}|\\uparrow \\uparrow \\rangle \\\\{\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}(|\\uparrow \\downarrow \\rangle +|\\downarrow \\uparrow \\rangle )\\\\|\\downarrow \\downarrow \\rangle \\end{array}}\\right)}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\left({\\begin{array}{ll}|\\uparrow \\uparrow \\rangle \\\\{\\frac {1}{\\sqrt {2}}}(|\\uparrow \\downarrow \\rangle +|\\downarrow \\uparrow \\rangle )\\\\|\\downarrow \\downarrow \\rangle \\end{array}}\\right)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4f2d5911a55b716b2f9482d8f47b0ea8c1eab1d2" style="vertical-align: -4.662ex; margin-bottom: -0.176ex; width:21.538ex; height:10.843ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>and thus consists of three types of nuclei, which are supposed to be symmetric: a deuterium nucleus (actually a highly <a href="/wiki/Excited_state" title="Excited state">excited state</a> of it), a nucleus with two protons, and a nucleus with two neutrons. These states are not stable.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Approximated_wavefunction_of_the_deuteron">Approximated wavefunction of the deuteron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Approximated wavefunction of the deuteron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The deuteron wavefunction must be antisymmetric if the isospin representation is used (since a proton and a neutron are not identical particles, the wavefunction\nneed not be antisymmetric in general). Apart from their isospin, the two nucleons also have spin and spatial distributions of their wavefunction. The latter is symmetric if the deuteron is symmetric under <a href="/wiki/Parity_(physics)" title="Parity (physics)">parity</a> (i.e. have an "even" or "positive" parity), and antisymmetric if the deuteron is antisymmetric under parity (i.e. have an "odd" or "negative" parity). The parity is fully determined by the total orbital angular momentum of the two nucleons: if it is even then the parity is even (positive), and if it is odd then the parity is odd (negative).\n</p><p>The deuteron, being an isospin singlet, is antisymmetric under nucleons exchange due to isospin, and therefore must be symmetric under the double exchange of their spin and location. Therefore, it can be in either of the following two different states:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Symmetric spin and symmetric under parity. In this case, the exchange of the two nucleons will multiply the deuterium wavefunction by (&#8722;1) from isospin exchange, (+1) from spin exchange and (+1) from parity (location exchange), for a total of (&#8722;1) as needed for antisymmetry.</li>\n<li>Antisymmetric spin and antisymmetric under parity. In this case, the exchange of the two nucleons will multiply the deuterium wavefunction by (&#8722;1) from isospin exchange, (&#8722;1) from spin exchange and (&#8722;1) from parity (location exchange), again for a total of (&#8722;1) as needed for antisymmetry.</li></ul>\n<p>In the first case the deuteron is a spin triplet, so that its total spin <i>s</i> is 1. It also has an even parity and therefore even orbital angular momentum <i>l</i>&#160;; The lower its orbital angular momentum, the lower its energy. Therefore, the lowest possible energy state has <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span>.\n</p><p>In the second case the deuteron is a spin singlet, so that its total spin <i>s</i> is 0. It also has an odd parity and therefore odd orbital angular momentum <i>l</i>. Therefore, the lowest possible energy state has <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 0</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 1</span>.\n</p><p>Since <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span> gives a stronger nuclear attraction, the deuterium <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a> is in the <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> =1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span> state.\n</p><p>The same considerations lead to the possible states of an isospin triplet having <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 0</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = even</span> or <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = odd</span>. Thus the state of lowest energy has <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 1</span>, higher than that of the isospin singlet.\n</p><p>The analysis just given is in fact only approximate, both because isospin is not an exact symmetry, and more importantly because the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_nuclear_interaction" title="Strong nuclear interaction">strong nuclear interaction</a> between the two nucleons is related to <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spin-orbit_interaction" title="Spin-orbit interaction">spin-orbit interaction</a> that mixes different <i>s</i> and <i>l</i> states. That is, <i>s</i> and <i>l</i> are not constant in time (they do not <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commutativity" title="Commutativity">commute</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Hamiltonian_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Hamiltonian (quantum mechanics)">Hamiltonian</a>), and over time a state such as <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span> may become a state of <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 2</span>. Parity is still constant in time so these do not mix with odd <i>l</i> states (such as <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 0</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 1</span>). Therefore, the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_state" title="Quantum state">quantum state</a> of the deuterium is a <a href="/wiki/Quantum_superposition" title="Quantum superposition">superposition</a> (a linear combination) of the <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span> state and the <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 2</span> state, even though the first component is much bigger. Since the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Total_angular_momentum" title="Total angular momentum">total angular momentum</a> <i>j</i> is also a good <a href="/wiki/Quantum_number" title="Quantum number">quantum number</a> (it is a constant in time), both components must have the same <i>j</i>, and therefore <span class="nowrap"><i>j</i> = 1</span>. This is the total spin of the deuterium nucleus.\n</p><p>To summarize, the deuterium nucleus is antisymmetric in terms of isospin, and has spin 1 and even (+1) parity. The relative angular momentum of its nucleons <i>l</i> is not well defined, and the deuteron is a superposition of mostly <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span> with some <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 2</span>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_and_electric_multipoles">Magnetic and electric multipoles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Magnetic and electric multipoles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In order to find theoretically the deuterium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole_moment" title="Magnetic dipole moment">magnetic dipole moment</a> &#181;, one uses the formula for a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_moment" title="Nuclear magnetic moment">nuclear magnetic moment</a>\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mu ={\\frac {1}{j+1}}\\langle (l,s),j,m_{j}=j|{\\vec {\\mu }}\\cdot {\\vec {\\jmath }}\\,|(l,s),j,m_{j}=j\\rangle }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#956;<!-- &#956;
