--></mo>\n<mi>s</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>s</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo maxsize="1.2em" minsize="1.2em">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n</mrow>\n<mo>]</mo>\n</mrow>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mu ={\\frac {1}{4(j+1)}}\\left[({g^{(s)}}_{p}+{g^{(s)}}_{n}){\\big (}j(j+1)-l(l+1)+s(s+1){\\big )}+{\\big (}j(j+1)+l(l+1)-s(s+1){\\big )}\\right]}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\mu ={\\frac {1}{4(j+1)}}\\left[({g^{(s)}}_{p}+{g^{(s)}}_{n}){\\big (}j(j+1)-l(l+1)+s(s+1){\\big )}+{\\big (}j(j+1)+l(l+1)-s(s+1){\\big )}\\right]}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a3c23357e4baf596b679ef024306fab09a5396a8" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:93.882ex; height:6.009ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>For the <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span> state (<span class="nowrap"><i>j</i> = 1</span>), we obtain\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mu ={\\frac {1}{2}}({g^{(s)}}_{p}+{g^{(s)}}_{n})=0.879}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#956;<!-- &#956; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>s</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>p</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>s</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>0.879</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mu ={\\frac {1}{2}}({g^{(s)}}_{p}+{g^{(s)}}_{n})=0.879}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\mu ={\\frac {1}{2}}({g^{(s)}}_{p}+{g^{(s)}}_{n})=0.879}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f46a3286c109b89e605f736374f7bfe602c3f68f" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:28.623ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>For the <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 2</span> state (<span class="nowrap"><i>j</i> = 1</span>), we obtain\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle \\mu =-{\\frac {1}{4}}({g^{(s)}}_{p}+{g^{(s)}}_{n})+{\\frac {3}{4}}=0.310}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>&#956;<!-- &#956; --></mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>s</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>p</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<msup>\n<mi>g</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>s</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>3</mn>\n<mn>4</mn>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>0.310</mn>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\mu =-{\\frac {1}{4}}({g^{(s)}}_{p}+{g^{(s)}}_{n})+{\\frac {3}{4}}=0.310}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle \\mu =-{\\frac {1}{4}}({g^{(s)}}_{p}+{g^{(s)}}_{n})+{\\frac {3}{4}}=0.310}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/77be671b87e9e4b25b0d3dbc67318c059c72a770" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:35.27ex; height:5.176ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>The measured value of the deuterium <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_dipole_moment" title="Magnetic dipole moment">magnetic dipole moment</a>, is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6993856999999999999&#9824;</span>0.857&#160;&#181;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left"><br/>N</span></span>, which is 97.5% of the <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6993879000000000000&#9824;</span>0.879&#160;&#181;<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.2em;font-size:85%;text-align:left"><br/>N</span></span> value obtained by simply adding moments of the proton and neutron. This suggests that the state of the deuterium is indeed to a good approximation <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span> state, which occurs with both nucleons spinning in the same direction, but their magnetic moments subtracting because of the neutron\'s negative moment.\n</p><p>But the slightly lower experimental number than that which results from simple addition of proton and (negative) neutron moments shows that deuterium is actually a linear combination of mostly <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span> state with a slight admixture of <span class="nowrap"><i>s</i> = 1</span>, <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 2</span> state.\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_dipole" title="Electric dipole">electric dipole</a> is zero <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_shell_model" title="Nuclear shell model">as usual</a>.\n</p><p>The measured electric <a href="/wiki/Quadrupole" title="Quadrupole">quadrupole</a> of the deuterium is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999285900000000000&#9824;</span>0.2859&#160;<a href="/wiki/Elementary_charge" title="Elementary charge">e</a>&#183;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Femtometer" title="Femtometer">fm</a><sup>2</sup></span>. While the order of magnitude is reasonable, since the deuterium radius is of order of 1 femtometer (see below) and its <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charge</a> is e, the above model does not suffice for its computation. More specifically, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_quadrupole" title="Electric quadrupole">electric quadrupole</a> does not get a contribution from the <i>l</i> =0 state (which is the dominant one) and does get a contribution from a term mixing the <i>l</i> =0 and the <i>l</i> =2 states, because the electric quadrupole <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operator_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Operator (quantum mechanics)">operator</a> does not <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Commutator_(quantum_mechanics)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Commutator (quantum mechanics) (page does not exist)">commute</a> with <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a>.\n</p><p>The latter contribution is dominant in the absence of a pure <span class="nowrap"><i>l</i> = 0</span> contribution, but cannot be calculated without knowing the exact spatial form of the nucleons <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wavefunction" title="Wavefunction">wavefunction</a> inside the deuterium.\n</p><p>Higher magnetic and electric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multipole_moment" title="Multipole moment">multipole moments</a> cannot be calculated by the above model, for similar reasons.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Deuterium has a number of commercial and scientific uses. These include:\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_reactors">Nuclear reactors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Nuclear reactors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_Ionized.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a1/Deuterium_Ionized.JPG/220px-Deuterium_Ionized.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a1/Deuterium_Ionized.JPG/330px-Deuterium_Ionized.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a1/Deuterium_Ionized.JPG/440px-Deuterium_Ionized.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_Ionized.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ionized deuterium in a <a href="/wiki/Fusor" title="Fusor">fusor</a> reactor giving off its characteristic pinkish-red glow</div></div></div>\n<p>Deuterium is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_water_reactor" title="Heavy water reactor">heavy water moderated fission reactors</a>, usually as liquid D<sub>2</sub>O, to slow neutrons without the high neutron absorption of ordinary hydrogen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This is a common commercial use for larger amounts of deuterium.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Research_reactor" title="Research reactor">research reactors</a>, liquid D<sub>2</sub> is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_source" title="Cold source">cold sources</a> to moderate neutrons to very low energies and wavelengths appropriate for <a href="/wiki/Neutron_scattering" title="Neutron scattering">scattering experiments</a>.\n</p><p>Experimentally, deuterium is the most common nuclide used in <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a> reactor designs, especially in combination with <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a>, because of the large reaction rate (or <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_cross_section" title="Nuclear cross section">nuclear cross section</a>) and high <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> yield of the D&#8211;T reaction. There is an even higher-yield D&#8211;<a href="/wiki/Helium-3" title="Helium-3"><span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span></a> fusion reaction, though the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Breakeven" title="Breakeven">breakeven</a> point of D&#8211;<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> is higher than that of most other fusion reactions; together with the scarcity of <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span>He<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, this makes it implausible as a practical power source until at least D&#8211;T and D&#8211;D fusion reactions have been performed on a commercial scale. However, commercial nuclear fusion is not yet an accomplished technology.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NMR_spectroscopy">NMR spectroscopy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: NMR spectroscopy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_NMR" title="Proton NMR">Proton NMR</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deuterium_NMR" title="Deuterium NMR">Deuterium NMR</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_lamp_1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3900" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Deuterium_lamp_1.png/220px-Deuterium_lamp_1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Deuterium_lamp_1.png/330px-Deuterium_lamp_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Deuterium_lamp_1.png/440px-Deuterium_lamp_1.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deuterium_lamp_1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Emission spectrum of an ultraviolet <a href="/wiki/Deuterium_arc_lamp" title="Deuterium arc lamp">deuterium arc lamp</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Deuterium is most commonly used in hydrogen <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance_spectroscopy" title="Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy">nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proton_NMR" title="Proton NMR">proton NMR</a>) in the following way. NMR ordinarily requires compounds of interest to be analyzed as dissolved in solution. Because of deuterium\'s nuclear spin properties which differ from the light hydrogen usually present in organic molecules, NMR spectra of hydrogen/protium are highly differentiable from that of deuterium, and in practice deuterium is not "seen" by an NMR instrument tuned for light-hydrogen. Deuterated solvents (including heavy water, but also compounds like deuterated chloroform, CDCl<sub>3</sub>) are therefore routinely used in NMR spectroscopy, in order to allow only the light-hydrogen spectra of the compound of interest to be measured, without solvent-signal interference.\n</p><p>Nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy can also be used to obtain information about the deuteron\'s environment in isotopically labelled samples (<a href="/wiki/Deuterium_NMR" title="Deuterium NMR">Deuterium NMR</a>). For example, the flexibility in the tail, which is a long hydrocarbon chains, in deuterium-labelled lipid molecules can be quantified using solid state deuterium NMR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Deuterium NMR spectra are especially informative in the solid state because of its relatively small quadrupole moment in comparison with those of bigger quadrupolar nuclei such as chlorine-35, for example.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tracing">Tracing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Tracing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_sciences" title="Environmental sciences">environmental sciences</a>, deuterium is used as a non-radioactive, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_isotope_biogeochemistry" title="Hydrogen isotope biogeochemistry">stable isotopic tracer</a>, for example, in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doubly_labeled_water_test" title="Doubly labeled water test">doubly labeled water test</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reactions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a>, deuterium behaves somewhat similarly to ordinary hydrogen (with a few chemical differences, as noted). It can be distinguished from ordinary hydrogen most easily by its mass, using <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infrared_spectrometry" title="Infrared spectrometry">infrared spectrometry</a>. Deuterium can be detected by <a href="/wiki/Femtosecond" title="Femtosecond">femtosecond</a> <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">infrared</a> spectroscopy, since the mass difference drastically affects the frequency of molecular vibrations; deuterium-carbon bond vibrations are found in spectral regions free of other signals.\n</p><p>Measurements of small variations in the natural abundances of deuterium, along with those of the stable heavy oxygen isotopes <sup>17</sup>O and <sup>18</sup>O, are of importance in <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrology</a>, to trace the geographic origin of Earth\'s waters. The heavy isotopes of hydrogen and oxygen in rainwater (so-called <a href="/wiki/Meteoric_water" title="Meteoric water">meteoric water</a>) are enriched as a function of the environmental temperature of the region in which the precipitation falls (and thus enrichment is related to mean latitude). The relative enrichment of the heavy isotopes in rainwater (as referenced to mean ocean water), when plotted against temperature falls predictably along a line called the <a href="/wiki/Global_meteoric_water_line" title="Global meteoric water line">global meteoric water line</a> (GMWL). This plot allows samples of precipitation-originated water to be identified along with general information about the climate in which it originated. Evaporative and other processes in bodies of water, and also ground water processes, also differentially alter the ratios of heavy hydrogen and oxygen isotopes in fresh and salt waters, in characteristic and often regionally distinctive ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The ratio of concentration of <sup>2</sup>H to <sup>1</sup>H is usually indicated with a delta as &#948;<sup>2</sup>H and the geographic patterns of these values are plotted in maps termed as isoscapes. Stable isotope are incorporated into plants and animals and an analysis of the ratios in a migrant bird or insect can help suggest a rough guide to their origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contrast_properties">Contrast properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Contrast properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Neutron_scattering" title="Neutron scattering">Neutron scattering</a> techniques particularly profit from availability of deuterated samples: The H and D cross sections are very distinct and different in sign, which allows contrast variation in such experiments. Further, a nuisance problem of ordinary hydrogen is its large incoherent neutron cross section, which is nil for D. The substitution of deuterium atoms for hydrogen atoms thus reduces scattering noise.\n</p><p>Hydrogen is an important and major component in all materials of organic chemistry and life science, but it barely interacts with X-rays. As hydrogen (and deuterium) interact strongly with neutrons, neutron scattering techniques, together with a modern deuteration facility,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> fills a niche in many studies of macromolecules in biology and many other areas.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_weapons">Nuclear weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Nuclear weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>This is discussed below. It is notable that although most stars, including the Sun, generate energy over most of their lives by fusing hydrogen into heavier elements, such fusion of light hydrogen (protium) has never been successful in the conditions attainable on Earth. Thus, all artificial fusion, including the hydrogen fusion that occurs in so-called hydrogen bombs, requires heavy hydrogen (either tritium or deuterium, or both) in order for the process to work.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drugs">Drugs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Drugs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Deuterated_drug" title="Deuterated drug">deuterated drug</a></div>\n<p>A deuterated drug is a <a href="/wiki/Small_molecule" title="Small molecule">small molecule</a> medicinal product in which one or more of the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> atoms contained in the drug molecule have been replaced by deuterium. Because of the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_isotope_effect" title="Kinetic isotope effect">kinetic isotope effect</a>, deuterium-containing drugs may have significantly lower rates of <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, and hence a longer <a href="/wiki/Biological_half-life" title="Biological half-life">half-life</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19295573_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19295573-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid23744136_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid23744136-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24294889_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24294889-33">[33]</a></sup>  In 2017, <a href="/wiki/Deutetrabenazine" title="Deutetrabenazine">deutetrabenazine</a> became the first deuterated drug to receive FDA approval.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reinforced_essential_nutrients">Reinforced essential nutrients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Reinforced essential nutrients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Deuterium can be used to reinforce specific oxidation-vulnerable C-H bonds within essential or conditionally <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_nutrients" title="Essential nutrients">essential nutrients</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> such as certain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acids" title="Amino acids">amino acids</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fatty_acid" title="Polyunsaturated fatty acid">polyunsaturated fatty acids</a> (PUFA), making them more resistant to oxidative damage. <a href="/wiki/Deuterated_drug" title="Deuterated drug">Deuterated</a> polyunsaturated <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acids</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Linoleic_acid" title="Linoleic acid">linoleic acid</a>, slow down the chain reaction of <a href="/wiki/Lipid_peroxidation" title="Lipid peroxidation">lipid peroxidation</a> that damage living cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Deuterated ethyl ester of linoleic acid (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RT001" title="RT001">RT001</a>), developed by Retrotope, is in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compassionate_use_trial" title="Compassionate use trial">compassionate use trial</a> in <a href="/wiki/Infantile_neuroaxonal_dystrophy" title="Infantile neuroaxonal dystrophy">infantile neuroaxonal dystrophy</a> and has successfully completed a Phase I/II trial in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedreich%E2%80%99s_ataxia" title="Friedreich&#8217;s ataxia">Friedreich&#8217;s ataxia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suspicion_of_lighter_element_isotopes">Suspicion of lighter element isotopes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Suspicion of lighter element isotopes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The existence of nonradioactive isotopes of lighter elements had been suspected in studies of neon as early as 1913, and proven by mass spectrometry of light elements in 1920. The prevailing theory at the time was that isotopes of an element differ by the existence of additional <i>protons</i> in the nucleus accompanied by an equal number of  <i><a href="/wiki/Discovery_of_the_neutron#Problems_of_the_nuclear_electrons_hypothesis" title="Discovery of the neutron">nuclear electrons</a></i>. In this theory, the deuterium nucleus with mass two and charge one would contain two protons and one nuclear electron. However it was expected that the element hydrogen with a measured average atomic mass very close to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000100000000000000&#9824;</span>1&#160;u</span>, the known mass of the proton, always has a nucleus composed of a single proton (a known particle), and could not contain a second proton. Thus, hydrogen could have no heavy isotopes.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deuterium_detected">Deuterium detected</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Deuterium detected">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Urey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="280" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Urey.jpg/170px-Urey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Urey.jpg/255px-Urey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/29/Urey.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Urey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Harold Urey</div></div></div>\n<p>It was first detected spectroscopically in late 1931 by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Urey" title="Harold Urey">Harold Urey</a>, a chemist at <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">Columbia University</a>. Urey\'s collaborator, <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Brickwedde" title="Ferdinand Brickwedde">Ferdinand Brickwedde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Distillation" title="Distillation">distilled</a> five <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liter" title="Liter">liters</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenically produced</a> <a href="/wiki/Liquid_hydrogen" title="Liquid hydrogen">liquid hydrogen</a> to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6994100000000000000&#9824;</span>1&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millilitre" title="Millilitre">mL</a></span> of liquid, using the low-temperature physics laboratory that had recently been established at the National Bureau of Standards in Washington, D.C. (now the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Standards_and_Technology" title="National Institute of Standards and Technology">National Institute of Standards and Technology</a>). The technique had previously been used to isolate heavy isotopes of neon. The cryogenic boiloff technique concentrated the fraction of the mass-2 isotope of hydrogen to a degree that made its spectroscopic identification unambiguous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_of_the_isotope_and_Nobel_Prize">Naming of the isotope and Nobel Prize</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Naming of the isotope and Nobel Prize">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Urey created the names protium, deuterium, and tritium in an article published in 1934. The name is based in part on advice from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G._N._Lewis" title="G. N. Lewis">G. N. Lewis</a> who had proposed the name "deutium". The name is derived from the,  Greek deuteros (second), and the nucleus to be called "deuteron" or "deuton". Isotopes and new elements were traditionally given the name that their discoverer decided. Some British scientists, such as <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Rutherford" title="Ernest Rutherford">Ernest Rutherford</a>, wanted the isotope to be called "diplogen", from the Greek diploos (double), and the nucleus to be called diplon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diplogen_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-diplogen-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The amount inferred for normal abundance of this heavy isotope of hydrogen was so small (only about 1 atom in 6400 hydrogen atoms in ocean water (156 deuteriums per million hydrogens)) that it had not noticeably affected previous measurements of (average) hydrogen atomic mass. This explained why it hadn\'t been experimentally suspected before. Urey was able to concentrate water to show partial enrichment of deuterium. Lewis had prepared the first samples of pure heavy water in 1933. The discovery of deuterium, coming before the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutron</a> in 1932, was an experimental shock to theory, but when the neutron was reported, making deuterium\'s existence more explainable, deuterium won Urey the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> in 1934. Lewis was embittered by being passed over for this recognition given to his former student.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diplogen_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-diplogen-2">[2]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id=".22Heavy_water.22_experiments_in_World_War_II"></span><span class="mw-headline" id=\'"Heavy_water"_experiments_in_World_War_II\'>"Heavy water" experiments in World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title=\'Edit section: "Heavy water" experiments in World War II\'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">Heavy water</a></div>\n<p>Shortly before the war, <a href="/wiki/Hans_von_Halban" title="Hans von Halban">Hans von Halban</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lew_Kowarski" title="Lew Kowarski">Lew Kowarski</a> moved their research on neutron moderation from France to England, smuggling the entire global supply of heavy water (which had been made in Norway) across in twenty-six steel drums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> was known to be conducting experiments using heavy water as moderator for a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">nuclear reactor</a> design. Such experiments were a source of concern because they might allow them to produce <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> for an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a>. Ultimately it led to the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allied</a> operation called the "<a href="/wiki/Norwegian_heavy_water_sabotage" title="Norwegian heavy water sabotage">Norwegian heavy water sabotage</a>", the purpose of which was to destroy the <a href="/wiki/Vemork" title="Vemork">Vemork</a> deuterium production/enrichment facility in Norway. At the time this was considered important to the potential progress of the war.\n</p><p>After World War II ended, the Allies discovered that Germany was not putting as much serious effort into the program as had been previously thought. They had been unable to sustain a chain reaction. The Germans had completed only a small, partly built experimental reactor (which had been hidden away). By the end of the war, the Germans did not even have a fifth of the amount of heavy water needed to run the reactor,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="why did they need heavy water to run a reactor? (February 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> partially due to the Norwegian heavy water sabotage operation. However, even had the Germans succeeded in getting a reactor operational (as the U.S. did with a graphite reactor in late 1942), they would still have been at least several years away from development of an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a> with maximal effort. The engineering process, even with maximal effort and funding, required about two and a half years (from first critical reactor to bomb) in both the U.S. and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S.S.R." title="U.S.S.R.">U.S.S.R.</a>, for example.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_thermonuclear_weapons">In thermonuclear weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: In thermonuclear weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_weapon" title="Thermonuclear weapon">Thermonuclear weapon</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ivy_Mike_Sausage_device.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1166" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Ivy_Mike_Sausage_device.jpg/330px-Ivy_Mike_Sausage_device.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Ivy_Mike_Sausage_device.jpg/495px-Ivy_Mike_Sausage_device.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Ivy_Mike_Sausage_device.jpg/660px-Ivy_Mike_Sausage_device.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ivy_Mike_Sausage_device.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A view of the <i>Sausage</i> device casing of the <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a>, with its instrumentation and cryogenic equipment attached. This bomb held a cryogenic Dewar flask containing room for as much as 160 kilograms of liquid deuterium. The bomb was 20 feet tall. Note the seated man at the right of the photo for the scale.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 62-ton <a href="/wiki/Ivy_Mike" title="Ivy Mike">Ivy Mike</a> device built by the United States and exploded on 1 November 1952, was the first fully successful "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a>" or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_bomb" title="Thermonuclear bomb">thermonuclear bomb</a>. In this context, it was the first bomb in which most of the energy released came from <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reaction" title="Nuclear reaction">nuclear reaction</a> stages that followed the primary <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">nuclear fission</a> stage of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a>. The Ivy Mike bomb was a factory-like building, rather than a deliverable weapon. At its center, a very large cylindrical, insulated <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_flask" title="Vacuum flask">vacuum flask</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cryostat" title="Cryostat">cryostat</a>, held <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryogenic" title="Cryogenic">cryogenic</a> liquid deuterium in a volume of about 1000 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liter" title="Liter">liters</a> (160 kilograms in mass, if this volume had been completely filled). Then, a conventional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_bomb" title="Atomic bomb">atomic bomb</a> (the "primary") at one end of the bomb was used to create the conditions of extreme temperature and pressure that were needed to set off the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_reaction" title="Thermonuclear reaction">thermonuclear reaction</a>.\n</p><p>Within a few years, so-called "dry" hydrogen bombs were developed that did not need cryogenic hydrogen. Released information suggests that all <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_weapon" title="Thermonuclear weapon">thermonuclear weapons</a> built since then contain <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compounds</a> of deuterium and lithium in their secondary stages. The material that contains the deuterium is mostly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium_deuteride" title="Lithium deuteride">lithium deuteride</a>, with the lithium consisting of the isotope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium-6" title="Lithium-6">lithium-6</a>. When the lithium-6 is bombarded with fast <a href="/wiki/Neutron" title="Neutron">neutrons</a> from the atomic bomb, <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-3" title="Hydrogen-3">hydrogen-3</a>) is produced, and then the deuterium and the tritium quickly engage in <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_fusion" title="Thermonuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a>, releasing abundant energy, <a href="/wiki/Helium-4" title="Helium-4">helium-4</a>, and even more free neutrons.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_research">Modern research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Modern research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In August 2018, scientists announced the transformation of gaseous deuterium into a <a href="/wiki/Metallic_hydrogen" title="Metallic hydrogen">liquid metallic form</a>. This may help researchers better understand <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">giant gas planets</a>, such as Jupiter, Saturn and related <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">exoplanets</a>, since such planets are thought to contain a lot of liquid metallic hydrogen, which may be responsible for their observed powerful <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic fields</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180816_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180816-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SCI-20180816_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-SCI-20180816-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_for_elemental_deuterium">Data for elemental deuterium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Data for elemental deuterium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Formula: D<sub>2</sub> or <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrogen-2" title="Hydrogen-2"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">1</sub></span>H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></a></span><sub>2</sub>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Density: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999180000000000000&#9824;</span>0.180&#160;kg/m<sup>3</sup></span> at <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">STP</a> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>0&#160;&#176;C</span>, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7005101325000000000&#9824;</span>101.325&#160;kPa</span>).</li>\n<li>Atomic weight: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7000201410179260000&#9824;</span>2.014<span style="margin-left:.25em;">101</span><span style="margin-left:.25em;">7926</span>&#160;u</span>.</li>\n<li>Mean abundance in ocean water (from <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Standard_Mean_Ocean_Water" title="Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water">VSMOW</a>) 155.76 &#177; 0.1 ppm (a ratio of 1 part per approximately 6420 parts), that is, about <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6998150000000000000&#9824;</span>0.015%</span> of the atoms in a sample (by number, not weight)</li></ul>\n<p>Data at approximately <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001180000000000000&#9824;</span>18&#160;K</span> for D<sub>2</sub> (<a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a>):\n</p>\n<ul><li>Density:\n<ul><li>Liquid: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002162400000000000&#9824;</span>162.4&#160;kg/m<sup>3</sup></span></li>\n<li>Gas: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">6999452000000000000&#9824;</span>0.452&#160;kg/m<sup>3</sup></span></li></ul></li>\n<li>Viscosity: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7001126000000000000&#9824;</span>12.6&#160;<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pascal_second" title="Pascal second">&#181;Pa&#183;s</a></span> at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002300000000000000&#9824;</span>300&#160;K</span> (gas phase)</li>\n<li>Specific heat capacity at constant pressure <i>c<sub>p</sub></i>:\n<ul><li>Solid: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003295000000000000&#9824;</span>2950&#160;J/(kg&#183;K)</span></li>\n<li>Gas: <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7003520000000000000&#9824;</span>5200&#160;J/(kg&#183;K)</span></li></ul></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Antideuterium">Antideuterium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Antideuterium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>An <b>antideuteron</b> is the <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a> counterpart of the nucleus of deuterium, consisting of an <a href="/wiki/Antiproton" title="Antiproton">antiproton</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Antineutron" title="Antineutron">antineutron</a>. The antideuteron was first produced in 1965 at the <a href="/wiki/Proton_Synchrotron" title="Proton Synchrotron">Proton Synchrotron</a> at <a href="/wiki/CERN" title="CERN">CERN</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Alternating_Gradient_Synchrotron" title="Alternating Gradient Synchrotron">Alternating Gradient Synchrotron</a> at <a href="/wiki/Brookhaven_National_Laboratory" title="Brookhaven National Laboratory">Brookhaven National Laboratory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> A complete atom, with a <a href="/wiki/Positron" title="Positron">positron</a> orbiting the nucleus, would be called <i>antideuterium</i>, but as of 2005 antideuterium has not yet been created. The proposed symbol for antideuterium is <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span><span style="text-decoration:overline;">D</span><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>, that is, D with an overbar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_hydrogen" title="Isotopes of hydrogen">Isotopes of hydrogen</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">Nuclear fusion</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tokamak" title="Tokamak">Tokamak</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">Tritium</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Heavy_water" title="Heavy water">Heavy water</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Deuterium&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external free" href="http://www.nndc.bnl.gov/chart/reCenter.jsp?z=1&amp;n=1" rel="nofollow">http://www.nndc.bnl.gov/chart/reCenter.jsp?z=1&amp;n=1</a></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-diplogen-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-diplogen_2-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diplogen_2-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diplogen_2-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-diplogen_2-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation
